The Property Report
Deal of the Week: A High-Rising Exposure
By Maura Webber Sadovi
769 words
24 April 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
C8
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Investors who snapped up Taylor Morrison Home Corp.'s shares when it recently went public might want to start rooting for Toronto to skirt a condo bust.

The Arizona home builder is long on Toronto high-rises at an unusually volatile time for real estate in Canada's biggest city. Taylor Morrison, known in the U.S. for its higher-end single-family homes, expects to complete three high-rise projects this year and an additional four in the Toronto area by the end of 2016. The projects are under way as a record 237 condo towers, most taller than 10 stories, are rising in the city and region's skyline, according to RealNet Canada Inc., a real-estate research firm.

Taylor Morrison attracted enough investors to push the company to issue 20% more shares than expected. The shares are up about 6.7% from the initial public offering price, as the company became the latest home builder to tap Wall Street's growing confidence in the U.S. housing recovery.

But the stock's debut also shows the limits of the market's appetite for housing risk. Some analysts say investors' concern over the company's exposure to the frothy Toronto condo market weighed on the stock's pricing right out of the box.

"Taylor Morrison got a haircut because of Canada," said Jack Micenko, an analyst with Susquehanna Financial Group, who has a "positive" rating on the stock. Taylor-Morrison shares were at $23.48, down 27 cents, in 4 p.m. trading Tuesday on the New York Stock Exchange. "It's a choppy market."

Other companies in the home-building sector also have seen some recent volatility, though the Dow Jones U.S. Home Construction Index, which tracks public home builders, has risen 61.5% over the past 12 months.

As with many home builders, the outlook for Taylor Morrison has changed considerably in recent years. The company was a unit of British conglomerate Taylor Wimpey PLC until early 2011, when a group of U.S. private-equity funds acquired it for nearly $1 billion. The move was seen as an effort by Taylor Wimpey to retreat from the weak U.S. market.

Now the tables are turning. Taylor Morrison's U.S. markets, including some like Phoenix that were among the hardest-hit by the housing crisis, are expected to be its growth engines. In regulatory filings, Taylor Morrison said the average sales price for the company's houses with deposits on them in the U.S. are expected to rise about 20%, to $398,000, in the first quarter from the year-earlier, while in Canada the average sales price was expected to be roughly flat. Closings in the U.S. are expected to rise 96% in the U.S. but decline 33% in Canada.

A Taylor Morrison spokeswoman noted that Canada accounted for less than 20% of the company's net income last year. While the company has seen some generic slowing in Canada, she said much of the problem has been in the luxury condo market, while the company sells condos at a lower price point.

She also said builders in Canada have some legal protections that they don't have in the U.S., such as legal recourse should a buyer who has put down a deposit opt to walk away. That reduces the cancellation rates even if prices fall or buyers get cold feet. "In Miami you had all those people walking from buildings. In Canada you wouldn't see that mass exodus," the spokeswoman said. "There are fundamental differences in Canada that make it a safer place to build high-rises."

The city anchors a region of about five million that also is undergoing some changes that those fearing a condo-bust may not grasp, said George Carras, president of RealNet Canada. New land policy is reducing the amount of available parcels for single-family development in the region and pushing developers to build more-dense high-rise projects on remaining land, he said. At the same time, demand is increasing as the city's population rose to about 2.8 million last year.

The number of sales of new high-rise condo units in the Toronto region fell 7.2% in February to 952 from 1,026 a year-earlier, while prices fell 1.3% to 432,000 Canadian dollars (US$421,257), according to RealNet.

European Shares Jump

Property: Condos Race for Space
By Josh Barbanel
866 words
23 April 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A28
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

A century ago, a much-admired brick-and-limestone mansion was demolished on East 68th Street and Madison Avenue to make way for the latest innovation for the rich -- an 11-story Beaux-Arts building with many apartments larger than a typical townhouse in the neighborhood.

"The service is neither seen nor heard," gushed a report in the New York Herald in 1914, celebrating a separate wing for servants in each large apartment. In addition to bedrooms, there was a staff sitting room and dining room in the apartments. There were more maids rooms on the building's roof.

Now that building -- known as the Marquand for the owner of that old mansion, Henry G. Marquand, a founder and second president of the Metropolitan Museum of Art -- is being reinvented again on its 100th birthday and turned into lavishly finished condominiums.

The changes at the Marquand, at 11 E. 68th St., are part of a recent wave of half a dozen conversions of older buildings on the Upper East Side into condominiums, mainly into large, expensive apartments that have been reporting strong interest from wealthy buyers in recent months, during the rebound in the luxury market.

"The demand is extremely strong particularly for larger apartments," said Richard Wallgren, who is overseeing sales at two apartment buildings being converted to condos by Harry Macklowe, the real-estate developer and investor. At 737 Park Ave. at East 71st Street, he said more than 30% of the 19-story building is listed in contract, four months after a sales office opened. The building dates to 1940.

Another project, Carlton House, the conversion of what had been a 1950 hotel at 61st Street and Madison, about 20% of the 68 condos are in contract, according to the developers, Extell Development Co. and Angelo, Gordon & Co.

At the Marquand, apartments that had been sliced and diced into smaller rental units during leaner decades are being restored to their original size or more, but without the wing for servants. Kitchens once the hidden preserve of the staff are now front and center, with counters and walls of Calacatta marble.

"Instead of upstairs downstairs, you have galleries for art, large canvasses of art," said Lee Mindel, of Shelton, Mindel & Associates, an architect who redesigned the interiors. He said that the kitchens and the adjacent family rooms have been turned into "mini-apartments."

Now the rooftop maids rooms -- there were still 13 penthouse maids rooms listed in a certificate of occupancy in 1973 -- are being incorporated into two triplex penthouses with private terraces. The building, with dozens of bay windows, is divided into two wings built around a central courtyard that is being rebuilt with bluestone paving stones and gardens. It went on the market last week, with half-floors starting at $15 million. A model apartment is due to open next month.

The Marquand was once the site of three townhouses built for Mr. Marquand, a businessman who worked as a banker, a Wall Street broker and investor in railroads, according to an account published by the Met.

The elaborate Victorian houses were designed in the early 1880s by Richard M. Hunt, the architect who designed the facade of the Met. Mr. Marquand's 42-foot-wide corner home was celebrated for its elaborate design, its expensive furnishings, art collection and an iron conservatory that looked out over then-bucolic Madison Avenue. U.S. President Grover Cleveland lived for a time in one of the adjacent Marquand houses when he practiced law in the city after his firm term in the White House.

But after Mr. Marquand's death in 1902, the house, which cost more than $1 million to build and furnish, couldn't find a private buyer, and it was eventually sold to a group of investors for $500,000.

They then acquired the other two houses as well. The new building, the first multifamily structure on the block, was designed by architect Herbert Lucas, who also headed up the investor group. It was designed with ceiling heights of more than 10 feet, and incorporated shields with an M for Marquand cut into stone on the exterior.

Soon after, amid the labor shortages of World War I, the use of an army of household servants began to decline, and some of the apartments were chopped up for smaller rental units, Mr. Mindel said.

At the end of 2011, the building was sold for $170 million to two developers who split it up. Vornado Realty Trust took over a 100-foot-long prime retail space on Madison Avenue, while Ziel Feldman and HFZ Capital Group kept the apartments.

Mr. Feldman said the building has 44 apartments that will be eventually consolidated to between 25 and 30 condos. Nine apartments still have rent-regulated tenants, he said.

Back in 1913, the cost of acquiring the land and putting up 11 E. 68th St. was put at $1.6 million. Now Mr. Feldman's conversion plan has a total offering price of $342 million, state records show.

Abreast of the Market
U.S. Blue Chips Slip --- Soft Caterpillar Results, Home-Sales Report Pressure Markets
By Alexandra Scaggs
722 words
23 April 2013
The Wall Street Journal Asia
AWSJ
21
English
(c) 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. To see the edition in which this article appeared, click here http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns[http://awsj.com.hk/factiva-ns]

U.S. blue chips fell Monday, although the broader market was more resilient, as investors weighed disappointing results from Caterpillar and a surprise decline in existing home sales against good news from Asia and Europe.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average declined 52 points, or 0.4%, to 14496 in midday trading. Last week, the Dow fell 318 points, or 2.1%, for its biggest weekly point and percentage decline since the week ended June 1, 2012.

The Standard & Poor's 500-stock index fell less than 0.1% to 1555, while the Nasdaq Composite Index added eight points, or 0.3%, to 3214.

Markets are reflecting a "tug of war" between investors who expect growth and those who don't, said Frank Trotter, president with EverBank Direct and chief executive of EverBank Wealth Management.

Stocks extended losses after the National Association of Realtors reported a surprise decline in sales of previously owned homes. Existing-home sales declined 0.6% in March to a seasonally adjusted rate of 4.92 million.

"The expectation is that the housing sector is one of the tailwinds for the economy, so any report that suggests weakness or otherwise is going to be viewed pretty negatively," said Hank Smith, chief investment of Radnor, Pa.-based Haverford Trust, which manages $6.5 billion in assets.

Caterpillar reported results that fell short of analysts' estimates. The Dow component, seen as a bellwether for global economic growth, provided a downbeat full-year outlook, amid weakness in its mining business and expectations of continued slow global economic growth. Still, shares of the blue-chip machinery maker edged up 0.6%. The stock lost 5.4% last week in anticipation of weak results.

"At the end of the day, earnings are what's going to matter," said Dan Greenhaus, chief global strategist with BTIG LLC.

U.S. stocks made a short-lived attempt to stem last week's losses in early trading after finance ministers from the Group of 20 major economies signaled approval of the Bank of Japan's aggressive stimulus measures, and a deadlock in the Italian presidential election was cleared.

Apple shares gained 1.7%, after falling 9.1% last week to close at the lowest level since December 2011. The company's fiscal second-quarter results are due out after Tuesday's closing bell and analysts expect a sharp year-over-year drop in earnings as a result of weakening demand for iPhones and iPads.

Dow component General Electric slipped 2.2% after J.P. Morgan Chase cut its rating on the industrial conglomerate to "neutral." The bank had kept a "buy" rating on the stock since September 2009. On Friday, GE shed 4.1% after the company said Europe's weak economy could continue to hurt results.

Halliburton rallied 4.1% after reporting earnings that were well above forecasts.

Treasury bond prices edged higher amid investor caution over the economic outlook. The benchmark 10-year Treasury added 1/32, yielding 1.705%.

European Stocks

Shares rose broadly as the re-election of Italy's president, Giorgio Napolitano, fueled hopes that a new government could soon be in place.

The benchmark Stoxx 600 index rose 0.2% to 285.68. Italy's FTSE Mib advanced 1.7% to 16021.71.

Bank shares rallied. Italy's Banco Popolare added 4.5%, UniCredit gained 2.7% and Intesa Sanpaolo advanced 1.8%. Credit Agricole gained 1% and Societe Generale rose 0.9% in France.

Investment bank Natixis rose 4.3% after Credit Suisse lifted the French firm to "outperform" from "neutral."

Delhaize Group jumped 11% after the Belgian food retailer reported a 3.8% increase in first-quarter organic revenue growth.

Royal Philips Electronics sank 5.2% after the firm said it continues to see a slow first half of the year due to difficult market trends, particularly in Europe and the U.S.

Norwegian fertilizer firm Yara International lost 4% after reporting a 25% fall in first-quarter profit.

Royal Philips Electronics slipped 5.2% as first-quarter sales, especially in its health-care and lighting businesses, came in weaker than some analysts had expected.

Markets
Treasurys Rebound Ahead of Sales
By Min Zeng
490 words
22 April 2013
16:19
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Concerns over the economic outlook helped Treasury bonds claw back earlier price losses, but looming new debt sales kept the market's rebound in check.

The benchmark 10-year Treasury note was 2/32 higher in late-afternoon trade, yielding 1.696%. The yield was trading near a four-month low. Bond prices move inversely to their yields.

Demand for the safe-harbor asset initially fell after the Group of 20 industrialized and developing countries refrained from slamming Japan's aggressive monetary stimulus that has weakened the yen. Investors believe fresh liquidity from Japan, on the back of ongoing money-printing from the Federal Reserve, could boost growth prospects.

Treasury prices also lost some ground when Italy ended weeks of limbo and elected a president, fueling a rally in Italian and Spanish government bonds and boosting riskier assets.

But the bond market shifted in favor of bulls after Caterpillar Inc.'s first-quarter net profit fell 45% amid a broad-based revenue decline, while existing home sales in March posted an unexpected decline.

"There has been bottom fishing in Treasury bonds" driven by continued uneasiness on the economic situation in Europe and the U.S., said Mary Ann Hurley, vice president of trading in Seattle at D.A. Davidson & Co.

The prospects that the Bank of Japan's stimulus would drive Japanese investors, including pension funds and life insurance companies, to buy high-grade fixed-income assets overseas also lured in fresh buyers.

The 10-year Treasury yield is much higher than the 0.61% yield on 10-year Japanese government bonds.

"There is still a lot going on favoring Treasurys," said Hicham Hajhamou, director of Treasury trading at Pierpont Securities.

Upcoming new debt sales tempered the bond market's ascent.

The Treasury Department is scheduled to sell $99 billion in Treasury notes this week, including $35 billion in two-year notes Tuesday, $35 billion in five-year notes Wednesday and $29 billion in seven-year notes Thursday.

Treasury yields have dropped in recent weeks as concerns rose over the pace of economic recovery in the U.S. following disappointing data on employment, consumer spending and manufacturing.

The 10-year Treasury note's yield has dropped from 2.086% in March, the peak of this year so far. The yield now trades below 1.76%, where the security ended 2012.

Investors are worried that growth could lose traction again just like it did in the past few years, though some analysts argue a recovering housing market would boost growth prospects this year.

Bond yields would rise again if upcoming data renew optimism over the growth outlook. In this case, the Federal Reserve could start pulling back on buying Treasury bonds before the end of this year, which would remove a main support for bond bulls in holding down bond yields.

Commercial Property: Penn Station-Area Upgrades --- Vornado Plans to Bring Offices, 'More Contemporary' Retailers to the Area
By Anjali Athavaley
746 words
22 April 2013
The Wall Street Journal
J
A20
English
(Copyright (c) 2013, Dow Jones & Company, Inc.)

Vornado Realty Trust is planning to give makeovers to some of its buildings in the neighborhoods around Pennsylvania Station, an area that's been slowly improving but still falling far short of planners' dreams.

For years, city officials and landlords have had grand schemes for turning the area into a retail and entertainment Mecca surrounding a new Madison Square Garden and train station relocated into the Corinthian column-lined James A. Farley Post Office. But most of those plans have been either delayed or shelved.

Vornado, one of the biggest office and retail landlords in the area, is moving ahead on its own with more modest projects. The company has decided to convert its building at 7 W. 34th St. currently occupied by a home decor showrooms, back into an office building. The idea is partly to tap into high demand for space from technology and media companies that are expanding in many parts of the city.

Vornado also is working on an upgrade of the Hotel Pennsylvania, a 1,700-room lodging across Seventh Avenue from Penn Station. Vornado, until recently, was considering replacing the hotel with an office skyscraper, but the company opted against that move partly because office rents and demand aren't strong enough.

Instead, Vornado is in talks with operators and developers to bring in a hotel partner, perhaps one that would be connected with an international chain. The company also is buying out retailers. "We're in the process of turning over and buying out a lot of the retail tenants on the street so that we can populate them with better, more contemporary, more exciting tenants and restaurants all over the place," Vornado's chairman and chief executive Steven Roth told investors at a Citigroup Inc. conference in March.

Vornado executives declined to comment. But in recent weeks they have been telling investors some details of their plans. "There's the extraordinary opportunity to create value, change the culture in the retail and in the Hotel Pennsylvania," Mr. Roth said.

Brokers say the area has made progress but still has room for improvement. Tenants like H&M, Forever 21 and the Gap increasingly are showing up, says Faith Hope Consolo, chairman of the retail group at Douglas Elliman.

She says of Vornado, "They own that area. Ever since they came into that area, it has only gotten better."

But she points out that the Herald Square area has seen the most progress and that some neighborhoods further to the west need improvement. "It's a domino effect," she says.

In 2006, Mr. Roth told investors yesterday that the plan to remake Penn Station and move Madison Square Garden would add $1.2 billion in value to the company's nearly 7 million square feet of property in the area.

Vornado purchased the 450,000-square foot office building at 7 W. 34th St. in 2000 and began leasing it out to home decor companies as showroom space in 2005. The company's move to convert it back into office space comes as Vornado is winding down its furniture showroom division. Last year, the company said that Google Inc.'s Motorola Mobility would lease 572,000 square feet in Vornado's Chicago Merchandise Mart building.

Vornado said in a letter to investors earlier this month that average rents in Penn Plaza have steadily grown from $37.98 a square foot in 2003 to $51.16 a square foot in 2012. Vornado says it benefits from spillover from Chelsea and Park Avenue South, "which are flooded by tech firms and workers who don't wear ties."

Technology companies are looking for space around Penn Station because rents are rising in Midtown South neighborhoods like the Meatpacking District. "Pricing in Midtown South has gone up substantially over the past three years, and those tenants are starting to push further north looking for space," says Paul Walker, first vice president at brokerage firm CBRE Group Inc.

He pointed to the Setai hotel at 36th Street and Fifth Avenue and cocktail bar the Liberty on 35th Street as evidence of a transformation in the area near 7 W. 34th St. "It's becoming a more desirable place for the young companies to occupy," Mr. Walker said.

Real Estate
The House With a Surprise Inside; Behind the walls of that Tudor or Colonial, a dramatic contemporary may be lurking. Faced with strict codes and steep construction costs, more homeowners are installing modern interiors in old homes
By Nancy Keates
1762 words
19 April 2013
12:14
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2013 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.

Michael and Amy Cohen live in a classic, stately stone Colonial. Until you step inside, that is.

Following a recent remodel, the four-bedroom, three-story house, built in the 1920s in the West Mount Airy section of Philadelphia, has an electric-blue fireplace and a wall that doubles as a "found art" installation project, with multicolored plastic bread tags embedded in a large pane of clear Plexiglas. A custom-made living-room rug echoes the shapes and drips of a painting Mr. Cohen made in preschool and matches the Coca-Cola -red sofa.

"Brick and stone says 'home' to me. But I really like modern design. I never thought that exterior and interior had to agree," says Ms. Cohen, 48, a high-school and middle-school social-studies teacher.

It's a tough time to build new. Construction costs have risen sharply over the past year, while existing-home prices, though rising, are still about a third below their peak. As a result, the number of custom single-family homes built in the U.S. last year was less than half the number built in 2005, according to Robert Denk, senior economist at the National Association of Home Builders . And finding an existing contemporary or modern house can be difficult, especially in older cities and towns.

So what's a modern-design aficionado to do? Redo an older home. Remodeling spending is up 9% over last year, according to the Joint Center for Housing Studies at Harvard. And according to Sheila Schmitz, editor in chief of home-décor website Houzz , there has been an increase in popularity of the "transitional" look, which blends the comfort and warmth of traditional design with the clean profiles and understated colors of contemporary counterparts.

The concept of installing a modern interior inside a traditional home, long seen in Europe, is becoming particularly popular in older U.S. cities. In San Francisco, architect Stephen Verner of Aleck Wilson Architects says most of his clients want contemporary interiors even in the oldest homes.

Pittsburgh-based architect Lewko Korzeniwsky recently finished turning the traditional, Colonial-style floor plan of an old 4,600-square-foot French Normandy mansion into an open, contemporary interior, reducing the number of bedrooms to four from six and introducing floor-to-ceiling glass windows and recessed lighting. Mr. Korzeniwsky says he is getting many more requests for projects that keep the exteriors but modernize the interiors.

This "hybrid" phenomenon is particularly evident in Philadelphia. The city is full of old stone and brick homes. Construction costs are among the highest in the country, reflecting taxes, union labor rates and extensive regulations. And strict historical codes can make it difficult to change a house's exterior. Yet the way people live has become increasingly contemporary, say architects and designers, so more owners are demanding interior changes to incorporate bigger kitchens, cleaner lines and open floor plans.

The market has been responding positively. Bruce Benjamin, a real-estate agent at Plumer & Associates, says houses whose interiors have been renovated in a contemporary way appeal to buyers because they're a "refreshing" change and, as a result, have a higher resale value. He points to a 2,300-square-foot, 150-year-old townhouse that sold last summer for its asking price of $1.15 million even though it lacked parking, usually a must for that price category. Mr. Benjamin attributes the sale price to the home's modern interior.

"No one in their right mind would knock down one of these beautiful old houses," says Alan Metcalfe , the principal at Metcalfe Architecture & Design in Philadelphia who designed the remodel for the Cohens. "It is a matter of reorienting them to the way people live." Local architects estimate modern redos cost about $200 a square foot in Philadelphia, depending on the materials and scope of the work.

Built in 1800, Hank McNeil's Neo-Georgian 13,000-square-foot Rittenhouse Square mansion is red brick and cube-shaped, with rectangular double-hung windows all aligned and a red front door flanked by Corinthian columns. When Mr. McNeil bought it in 2003 for $1.3 million, it had been converted into law offices, with the interior rooms chopped up. Now, the interior is highly contemporary, and filled with one of the leading collections of minimalist modern art in the country.

Mr. McNeil, an heir to the Tylenol fortune, couldn't touch the exterior due to historical regulations. So he turned his attention inside, knocking down walls to create three rooms on the main floor. He installed glass doors, skylights and modern Bulthaup cabinets in the open kitchen.

The overall interior look of Mr. McNeil's house is sparse: He has a few pieces of furniture, mostly Danish, and simple rugs over the white oak floors. The focus is the art, which includes 14 major wall drawings by Sol LeWitt, 14 sculptures by Donald Judd and works by Carl Andre, Dan Flavin and Richard Tuttle. "People are really surprised when they come into my house. They're taken aback by the openness," says Mr. McNeil, who regularly hosts tours of museum board members from around the world.

Joan Wadleigh Curran opted for more of an industrial modern look when she turned three internally connected 19th-century carriage houses in the heart of Philadelphia's downtown into a 4,300-square-foot three bedroom home. An artist, Ms. Wadleigh Curran focused on keeping original details like the brick walls and unsealed concrete but added other natural materials like steel beams and an open, steel staircase to give it what she calls an "unfinished look."

"The building has a feel of history to it. It's like life—there's a history to things but there's also the new. I wanted to keep that juxtaposition," says Ms. Wadleigh Curran, who bought the house for $907,000 in 2010.

In Philadelphia, it makes sense to buy an old house: The city's housing stock is venerable, with a median year of construction of 1925. Besides, people love the way the old houses look. "You can get a very nice 2,500-square-foot Colonial, Queen Anne or Romanesque home in a beautiful, tree-lined neighborhood for $300,000 to $600,000," says Kevin Gillen , a senior research consultant at the Fels Institute of Government at the University of Pennsylvania . "Since you would pay at least double that in other markets, the savings on the price can be used to undertake significant rehabs of the interior."

Like a number of other cities, Philadelphia has also experienced an urban revitalization that is spurring greater interest in modern design. From 2006 through 2012, Philadelphia's population grew by 58,897 after falling for half a century, with 20- to 34-year-olds' share of the population up to 26% from 20%, according to Census estimates. Philadelphia also has a strong contemporary-design community, with seven college-level design programs as well as a number of new design-company startups and stores, that has influenced the type of interiors people want.

"The city is getting more sophisticated, but people don't want to forget about its past," says Stefan Sklaroff, who just opened a 10,000-square-foot, two-level modern-furniture store called Cella Luxuria in a 19th-century brick building downtown.

Yet unlike design-friendly cities such as New York, Miami and Milan, Philadelphia loathes showiness, reflecting its Quaker heritage. The contrast between the staid exteriors and the vibrant interiors of homes is a metaphor for the city, says Val Nehez, an interior designer who works both out of Philadelphia and New York. "Philadelphia doesn't wear its style on its sleeve. It's behind closed doors. You have to be in the fabric of society—to enter into peoples' lives—to see it."

Not everyone celebrates the demolition of details like wood moldings and stately fireplaces in favor of white Sheetrock, loftlike spaces and glass. While the interiors of homes are almost always up to the owners, Philadelphians aren't always shy about voicing their opinions.

Ms. Cohen, whose recently remodeled home includes the blue fireplace and red sofa, admits she was shocked by some nasty comments that were posted when photos of her house appeared on an online forum. Commenters described the inside as a "circus horror story," "like a clown threw up" and "like a day care." Wrote one: "I don't hate modern design, but the total mismatch between exterior and interior is disturbing. Imagine it reversed, modern exterior/traditional interior. It's not right, is it?" In response, Ms. Cohen says she told her two daughters: "Day cares are bright and cheerful places, and I'm sure it's quite lovely when a clown vomits."

Some owners try to merge the two sensibilities. It took art curator Eileen Tognini, 52, about 25 years—and her daughter moving out to go to college—before she and her husband finally turned the interior of their three-story, 2,500-square-foot Victorian 1882 brownstone in what's called the Fishtown neighborhood of Philadelphia into the contemporary design they'd long wanted. "Victorian has never been my sensibility, but I appreciated the bones," she says of the 10-month renovation, completed last June.

The result is very modern, particularly toward the back of the home: The dining room opens to the midcentury-modern and Japanese-influenced wood-and-steel kitchen, where double-panel cabinet doors are made from laser-cut steel and feature an abstract design modeled after a historic map of the neighborhood. There is a massive, wraparound glass staircase, and dropping down from the 24-foot-tall ceiling is a 12-foot-long "found art" light sculpture made by a local artist with steel rods and old trumpets, toy train tracks and parts of old pistons. But the couple decided to maintain the traditional feeling of the home's front rooms, retaining the original tile in the vestibule and the chestnut molding in the parlor. A Victorian mantle sits not too far from a second, newly installed glass-and-steel staircase.

"We felt really strongly it was important to preserve the history and not to assault that sensibility when you first walk in," Ms. Tognini says.

Write to Nancy Keates at nancy.keates@wsj.com [mailto:nancy.keates@wsj.com]

Dow Jones & Company, Inc.

"People are really surprised when they come into my house. They're taken aback by the openness," says Mr. McNeil, who regularly hosts tours of museum board members from around the world.

Joan Wadleigh Curran opted for more of an industrial modern look when she turned three internally connected 19th-century carriage houses in the heart of Philadelphia's downtown into a 4,300-square-foot three bedroom home. An artist, Ms. Wadleigh Curran focused on keeping original details like the brick walls and unsealed concrete but added other natural materials like steel beams and an open, steel staircase to give it what she calls an "unfinished look."

"The building has a feel of history to it. It's like life there's a history to things but there's also the new. I wanted to keep that juxtaposition," says Ms. Wadleigh Curran, who bought the house for $907,000 in 2010.

In Philadelphia, it makes sense to buy an old house: The city's housing stock is venerable, with a median year of construction of 1925. Besides, people love the way the old houses look. "You can get a very nice 2,500-square-foot Colonial, Queen Anne or Romanesque home in a beautiful, tree-lined neighborhood for $300,000 to $600,000," says Kevin Gillen , a senior research consultant at the Fels Institute of Government at the University of Pennsylvania . "Since you would pay at least double that in other markets, the savings on the price can be used to undertake significant rehabs of the interior."

Like a number of other cities, Philadelphia has also experienced an urban revitalization that is spurring greater interest in modern design. From 2006 through 2012, Philadelphia's population grew by 58,897 after falling for half a century, with 20- to 34-year-olds' share of the population up to 26% from 20%, according to Census estimates. Philadelphia also has a strong contemporary-design community, with seven college-level design programs as well as a number of new design-company startups and stores, that has influenced the type of interiors people want.

"The city is getting more sophisticated, but people don't want to forget about its past," says Stefan Sklaroff, who just opened a 10,000-square-foot, two-level modern-furniture store called Cella Luxuria in a 19th-century brick building downtown.

Yet unlike design-friendly cities such as New York, Miami and Milan, Philadelphia loathes showiness, reflecting its Quaker heritage. The contrast between the staid exteriors and the vibrant interiors of homes is a metaphor for the city, says Val Nehez, an interior designer who works both out of Philadelphia and New York. "Philadelphia doesn't wear its style on its sleeve. It's behind closed doors. You have to be in the fabric of society to enter into peoples' lives to see it."

Not everyone celebrates the demolition of details like wood moldings and stately fireplaces in favor of white Sheetrock, loftlike spaces and glass. While the interiors of homes are almost always up to the owners, Philadelphians aren't always shy about voicing their opinions.

Ms. Cohen, whose recently remodeled home includes the blue fireplace and red sofa, admits she was shocked by some nasty comments that were posted when photos of her house appeared on an online forum. Commenters described the inside as a "circus horror story," "like a clown threw up" and "like a day care." Wrote one: "I don't hate modern design, but the total mismatch between exterior and interior is disturbing. Imagine it reversed, modern exterior/traditional interior. It's not right, is it?" In response, Ms. Cohen says she told her two daughters: "Day cares are bright and cheerful places, and I'm sure it's quite lovely when a clown vomits."

Some owners try to merge the two sensibilities. It took art curator Eileen Tognini, 52, about 25 years -- and her daughter moving out to go to college -- before she and her husband finally turned the interior of their three-story, 2,500-square-foot Victorian 1882 brownstone in what's called the Fishtown neighborhood of Philadelphia into the contemporary design they'd long wanted. "Victorian has never been my sensibility, but I appreciated the bones," she says of the 10-month renovation, completed last June.

The result is very modern, particularly toward the back of the home: The dining room opens to the midcentury-modern and Japanese-influenced wood-and-steel kitchen, where double-panel cabinet doors are made from laser-cut steel and feature an abstract design modeled after a historic map of the neighborhood. There is a massive, wraparound glass staircase, and dropping down from the 24-foot-tall ceiling is a 12-foot-long "found art" light sculpture made by a local artist with steel rods and old trumpets, toy train tracks and parts of old pistons. But the couple decided to maintain the traditional feeling of the home's front rooms, retaining the original tile in the vestibule and the chestnut molding in the parlor. A Victorian mantle sits not too far from a second, newly installed glass-and-steel staircase.

"We felt really strongly it was important to preserve the history and not to assault that sensibility when you first walk in," Ms. Tognini says.
